<div class="description">Both performed by the Fulda Symphonic Orchestra, conductor: Simon Schindler</div></div></div></div>
<div class="side-box-abovebelow"><hr/><i class="selfreference">Problems playing these files? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></div>
</div>
<p>Mozart's music, like <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Haydn" title="Joseph Haydn">Haydn</a>'s, stands as an archetype of the <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">Classical style</a>. At the time he began composing, European music was dominated by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galant" title="Galant">style galant</a></i>, a reaction against the highly evolved intricacy of the <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">Baroque</a>. Progressively, and in large part at the hands of Mozart himself, the <a href="/wiki/Counterpoint" title="Counterpoint">contrapuntal</a> complexities of the late Baroque emerged once more, moderated and disciplined by new <a href="/wiki/Musical_form" title="Musical form">forms</a>, and adapted to a new aesthetic and social milieu. Mozart was a versatile composer, and wrote in every major genre, including <a href="/wiki/Symphony" title="Symphony">symphony</a>, opera, the solo concerto, chamber music including <a href="/wiki/String_quartet" title="String quartet">string quartet</a> and <a href="/wiki/String_quintet" title="String quintet">string quintet</a>, and the piano <a href="/wiki/Sonata" title="Sonata">sonata</a>. These forms were not new, but Mozart advanced their technical sophistication and emotional reach. He almost single-handedly developed and popularized the Classical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mozart_piano_concertos" title="Mozart piano concertos">piano concerto</a>. He wrote a great deal of <a href="/wiki/Religious_music" title="Religious music">religious music</a>, including large-scale <a href="/wiki/Mass_(music)" title="Mass (music)">masses</a>, as well as dances, <a href="/wiki/Divertimento" title="Divertimento">divertimenti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serenade" title="Serenade">serenades</a>, and other forms of light entertainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove1954[httpsarchiveorgdetailsgrovesdictionary01grovpage958_958–982]_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove1954[httpsarchiveorgdetailsgrovesdictionary01grovpage958_958–982]-111">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>The central traits of the Classical style are all present in Mozart's music. Clarity, balance, and transparency are the hallmarks of his work, but simplistic notions of its delicacy mask the exceptional power of his finest masterpieces, such as the <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._24_(Mozart)" title="Piano Concerto No. 24 (Mozart)">Piano Concerto No. 24</a> in C minor, K. 491; the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._40_(Mozart)" title="Symphony No. 40 (Mozart)">Symphony No. 40</a> in G minor, K. 550; and the opera <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Giovanni" title="Don Giovanni">Don Giovanni</a></i>. <a href="/wiki/Charles_Rosen" title="Charles Rosen">Charles Rosen</a> makes the point forcefully:
</p>
<blockquote><p>It is only through recognizing the violence and sensuality at the center of Mozart's work that we can make a start towards a comprehension of his structures and an insight into his magnificence. In a paradoxical way, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Schumann" title="Robert Schumann">Schumann</a>'s superficial characterization of the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._40_(Mozart)" title="Symphony No. 40 (Mozart)">G minor Symphony</a> can help us to see Mozart's daemon more steadily. In all of Mozart's supreme expressions of suffering and terror, there is something shockingly voluptuous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERosen1998324_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERosen1998324-112">[104]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>During his last decade, Mozart frequently exploited <a href="/wiki/Chromaticism" title="Chromaticism">chromatic</a> harmony. A notable instance is his <a href="/wiki/String_Quartet_No._19_(Mozart)" title="String Quartet No. 19 (Mozart)"><i>String Quartet in C major</i>, K. 465</a> (1785), whose introduction abounds in chromatic suspensions, giving rise to the work's nickname, the "Dissonance" quartet.
</p><p>Mozart had a gift for absorbing and adapting the valuable features of others' music. His travels helped in the forging of a unique compositional language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[105]</a></sup> In London as a child, he met <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christian_Bach" title="Johann Christian Bach">J. C. Bach</a> and heard his music. In Paris, Mannheim, and Vienna he met with other compositional influences, as well as the avant-garde capabilities of the <a href="/wiki/Mannheim_school" title="Mannheim school">Mannheim orchestra</a>. In Italy, he encountered the <a href="/wiki/Italian_overture" title="Italian overture">Italian overture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Opera_buffa" title="Opera buffa">opera buffa</a>, both of which deeply affected the evolution of his practice. In London and Italy, the galant style was in the ascendent: simple, light music with a mania for <a href="/wiki/Cadence" title="Cadence">cadencing</a>; an emphasis on tonic, dominant, and subdominant to the exclusion of other harmonies; symmetrical phrases; and clearly articulated partitions in the overall form of movements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeartz2003_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeartz2003-114">[106]</a></sup> Some of Mozart's early symphonies are <a href="/wiki/Italian_overture" title="Italian overture">Italian overtures</a>, with three movements running into each other; many are <a href="/wiki/Homotonal" title="Homotonal">homotonal</a> (all three movements having the same key signature, with the slow middle movement being in the <a href="/wiki/Relative_key" title="Relative key">relative minor</a>). Others mimic the works of J. C. Bach, and others show the simple <a href="/wiki/Binary_form" title="Binary form">rounded binary forms</a> turned out by Viennese composers.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:K626_Requiem_Dies_Irae.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1351" data-file-width="1868" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/K626_Requiem_Dies_Irae.jpg/220px-K626_Requiem_Dies_Irae.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/K626_Requiem_Dies_Irae.jpg/330px-K626_Requiem_Dies_Irae.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/K626_Requiem_Dies_Irae.jpg/440px-K626_Requiem_Dies_Irae.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:K626_Requiem_Dies_Irae.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Facsimile sheet of music from the Dies Irae movement of the <a href="/wiki/Requiem_(Mozart)" title="Requiem (Mozart)">Requiem Mass in D minor</a> (K. 626) in Mozart's handwriting (<a href="/wiki/Mozarthaus_Vienna" title="Mozarthaus Vienna">Mozarthaus</a>, Vienna)</div></div></div>
<p>As Mozart matured, he progressively incorporated more features adapted from the Baroque. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._29_(Mozart)" title="Symphony No. 29 (Mozart)">Symphony No. 29 in A major</a> K. 201 has a contrapuntal main theme in its first movement, and experimentation with irregular phrase lengths. Some of his quartets from 1773 have fugal finales, probably influenced by Haydn, who had included three such finales in his recently published Opus 20 set. The influence of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sturm_und_Drang" title="Sturm und Drang">Sturm und Drang</a></i> ("Storm and Stress") period in music, with its brief foreshadowing of the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic era</a>, is evident in the music of both composers at that time. Mozart's <a href="/wiki/Symphony_No._25_(Mozart)" title="Symphony No. 25 (Mozart)">Symphony No. 25 in G minor</a> K. 183 is another excellent example.
</p><p>Mozart would sometimes switch his focus between operas and instrumental music. He produced operas in each of the prevailing styles: <a href="/wiki/Opera_buffa" title="Opera buffa">opera buffa</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Marriage_of_Figaro" title="The Marriage of Figaro">The Marriage of Figaro</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Giovanni" title="Don Giovanni">Don Giovanni</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Cos%C3%AC_fan_tutte" title="Così fan tutte">Così fan tutte</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Opera_seria" title="Opera seria">opera seria</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Idomeneo" title="Idomeneo">Idomeneo</a></i>; and <a href="/wiki/Singspiel" title="Singspiel">Singspiel</a>, of which <i><a href="/wiki/The_Magic_Flute" title="The Magic Flute">Die Zauberflöte</a></i> is the most famous example by any composer. In his later operas, he employed subtle changes in instrumentation, orchestral texture, and <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">tone colour</a>, for emotional depth and to mark dramatic shifts. Here his advances in opera and instrumental composing interacted: his increasingly sophisticated use of the orchestra in the symphonies and concertos influenced his operatic orchestration, and his developing subtlety in using the orchestra to psychological effect in his operas was in turn reflected in his later non-operatic compositions.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinstein1965[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_July_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(July_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_115-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinstein1965[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_July_2020]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(July_2020)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-115'>[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="K.C3.B6chel_catalogue"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Köchel_catalogue">Köchel catalogue</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6chel_catalogue" title="Köchel catalogue">Köchel catalogue</a></div>
<p>For unambiguous identification of works by Mozart, a <i>Köchel catalogue number</i> is used. This is a unique number assigned, in regular chronological order, to every one of his known works. A work is referenced by the abbreviation "K." or "KV" followed by this number. The first edition of the catalogue was completed in 1862 by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Ritter_von_K%C3%B6chel" title="Ludwig Ritter von Köchel">Ludwig von Köchel</a>. It has since been repeatedly updated, as scholarly research improves knowledge of the dates and authenticity of individual works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZaslawCowdery1990331–332_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZaslawCowdery1990331–332-116">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Instruments">Instruments</span></h3>
<p>Although some of Mozart's early pieces were written for harpsichord, he also became acquainted in his early years with pianos made by <a href="/wiki/Regensburg" title="Regensburg">Regensburg</a> builder <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Franz_Jakob_Sp%C3%A4th&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Franz Jakob Späth (page does not exist)">Franz Jakob Späth</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franz_Jakob_Sp%C3%A4th" title="de:Franz Jakob Späth">de</a>]</span>. Later when Mozart was visiting Augsburg, he was impressed by <a href="/wiki/Johann_Andreas_Stein" title="Johann Andreas Stein">Stein</a> pianos and shared this in a letter to his father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup> On 22 October 1777, Mozart had premiered <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._7_(Mozart)" title="Piano Concerto No. 7 (Mozart)">his triple-piano concerto</a>, K. 242, on instruments provided by Stein. The <a href="/wiki/Augsburg_Cathedral" title="Augsburg Cathedral">Augsburg Cathedral</a> organist Demmler was playing the first, Mozart the second and Stein the third part.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[110]</a></sup> In 1783 when living in Vienna he purchased an instrument by <a href="/wiki/Anton_Walter" title="Anton Walter">Walter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup> Leopold Mozart confirmed the attachment which Mozart had with his Walter fortepiano: "It is impossible to describe the hustle and bustle. Your brother's pianoforte has been moved at least twelve times from his house to the theatre or to someone else's house."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frankfurt,_Mozartdenkmal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4288" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Frankfurt%2C_Mozartdenkmal.jpg/170px-Frankfurt%2C_Mozartdenkmal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Frankfurt%2C_Mozartdenkmal.jpg/255px-Frankfurt%2C_Mozartdenkmal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Frankfurt%2C_Mozartdenkmal.jpg/340px-Frankfurt%2C_Mozartdenkmal.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frankfurt,_Mozartdenkmal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mozart_Monument,_Frankfurt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mozart Monument, Frankfurt (page does not exist)">Mozart Monument</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mozart-Denkmal_(Frankfurt_am_Main)" title="de:Mozart-Denkmal (Frankfurt am Main)">de</a>]</span>, Mozartplatz, Frankfurt</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart_in_popular_culture" title="Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart in popular culture">Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart in popular culture</a></div>
<p>His most famous pupil, whom the Mozarts took into their Vienna home for two years as a child, was probably <a href="/wiki/Johann_Nepomuk_Hummel" title="Johann Nepomuk Hummel">Johann Nepomuk Hummel</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Transition_from_Classical_to_Romantic_music" title="Transition from Classical to Romantic music">transitional figure</a> between the Classical and Romantic eras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESolomon1995574_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESolomon1995574-121">[113]</a></sup> More important is the influence Mozart had on composers of later generations. Ever since the surge in his reputation after his death, studying his scores has been a standard part of classical musicians' training.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ludwig_van_Beethoven" title="Ludwig van Beethoven">Ludwig van Beethoven</a>, Mozart's junior by fifteen years, was deeply influenced by his work, with which he was acquainted as a teenager.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[115]</a></sup> He is thought to have performed Mozart's operas while playing in the court orchestra at Bonn<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE''Raptus_Association_for_Music_Appreciation''_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE''Raptus_Association_for_Music_Appreciation''-124">[116]</a></sup> and travelled to Vienna in 1787 hoping to study with the older composer. Some of <a href="/wiki/List_of_compositions_by_Ludwig_van_Beethoven#Instrumental_works:_WoO_1–86" title="List of compositions by Ludwig van Beethoven">Beethoven's works</a> have direct models in comparable works by Mozart, and he wrote <a href="/wiki/Cadenza" title="Cadenza">cadenzas</a> (<a href="/wiki/WoO" title="WoO">WoO</a> 58) to Mozart's D minor piano concerto <a href="/wiki/Piano_Concerto_No._20_(Mozart)" title="Piano Concerto No. 20 (Mozart)">K. 466</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChurgin1987457–458_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChurgin1987457–458-125">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[n 9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Composers have paid homage to Mozart by writing sets of <a href="/wiki/Variation_(music)" title="Variation (music)">variations</a> on his themes. Beethoven wrote four such sets (Op. 66, WoO 28, WoO 40, WoO 46).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChurgin1987458_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChurgin1987458-127">[118]</a></sup> Others include <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Sor" title="Fernando Sor">Fernando Sor</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Introduction_and_Variations_on_a_Theme_by_Mozart_(Sor)" title="Introduction and Variations on a Theme by Mozart (Sor)">Introduction and Variations on a Theme by Mozart</a> (1821), <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Glinka" title="Mikhail Glinka">Mikhail Glinka</a>'s Variations on a Theme from Mozart's Opera <i><a href="/wiki/The_Magic_Flute" title="The Magic Flute">The Magic Flute</a></i> (1822), <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Chopin" title="Frédéric Chopin">Frédéric Chopin</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Variations_on_%22L%C3%A0_ci_darem_la_mano%22_(Chopin)" title='Variations on "Là ci darem la mano" (Chopin)'>Variations on "Là ci darem la mano"</a> from <i><a href="/wiki/Don_Giovanni" title="Don Giovanni">Don Giovanni</a></i> (1827), and <a href="/wiki/Max_Reger" title="Max Reger">Max Reger</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Variations_and_Fugue_on_a_Theme_by_Mozart" title="Variations and Fugue on a Theme by Mozart">Variations and Fugue on a Theme by Mozart</a> (1914), based on the variation theme in the piano sonata <a href="/wiki/Piano_Sonata_No._11_(Mozart)" title="Piano Sonata No. 11 (Mozart)">K. 331</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMarchGreenfieldLayton2005_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMarchGreenfieldLayton2005-128">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Ilyich_Tchaikovsky" title="Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky">Pyotr Ilyich Tchaikovsky</a>, who revered Mozart, wrote his Orchestral Suite No. 4 in G, <i><a href="/wiki/Orchestral_Suite_No._4_Mozartiana_(Tchaikovsky)" title="Orchestral Suite No. 4 Mozartiana (Tchaikovsky)">Mozartiana</a></i> (1887), as a tribute to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sources vary in how Mozart's name should be pronounced in English. <a href="#CITEREFFradkin1996">Fradkin 1996</a>, a guide for radio announcers, strongly recommends <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ts]</span> for letter <i>z</i> (thus <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'w' in 'wind'">w</span><span title="/ʊ/: 'u' in 'push'">ʊ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="/ŋ/: 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ɑːr/: 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">WUULF</span>-gang <span style="font-size:90%">AM</span>-ə-<span style="font-size:90%">DAY</span>-əs <span style="font-size:90%">MOHT</span>-sart</i></a>), but otherwise considers English-like pronunciation fully acceptable. The German one is <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˈvɔlfɡaŋ ʔamaˈdeːʊs ˈmoːtsaʁt]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/90/De-Wolfgang_Amadeus_Mozart.ogg" title="De-Wolfgang Amadeus Mozart.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mozart's exact name involved many complications; for details, see <a href="/wiki/Mozart%27s_name" title="Mozart's name">Mozart's name</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Source: <a href="#CITEREFWilson1999">Wilson 1999</a>, p. 2. The many changes of European political borders since Mozart's time make it difficult to assign him an unambiguous nationality; for discussion, see <a href="/wiki/Mozart%27s_nationality" title="Mozart's nationality">Mozart's nationality</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFEisenKeefe2006">Eisen &amp; Keefe 2006</a>, p. 268: "You ask me to take the young Salzburger into your service. I do not know why not believing that you have need for a composer or of useless people.<span class="nowrap"> </span>... What I say is intended only to prevent you from burdening yourself with useless people and giving titles to people of that sort. In addition, if they are at your service, it degrades that service when these people go about the world like beggars."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Archbishop Colloredo responded to the request by dismissing both Mozart and his father, though the dismissal of the latter was not actually carried out.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mozart complains of this in a letter to his father, dated 24 March 1781.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpaethling2000235_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpaethling2000235-50">[45]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A more recent view, <a href="#CITEREFWolff2012">Wolff 2012</a>, is that Mozart's position was a more substantial one than is traditionally maintained, and that some of Mozart's chamber music from this time was written as part of his imperial duties.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">More recently, <a href="#CITEREFWolff2012">Wolff 2012</a> has forcefully advocated a view of Mozart's career at the end of his life as being on the rise, interrupted by his sudden death.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For further details, see <a href="/wiki/Beethoven_and_Mozart" title="Beethoven and Mozart">Beethoven and Mozart</a>.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
